21:30:10","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 13:30:10","title":"Microsoft’s new Flight Simulator is a beautiful work in progress","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2023914","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">For the last two weeks, I&#8217;ve been flying around the world in a preview of <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/microsoft\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"microsoft\">Microsoft&#8217;s <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> new <a href=\"https://www.flightsimulator.com/\">Flight Simulator</a>. Without a doubt, it&#8217;s the most beautiful flight simulator yet, and it&#8217;ll make you want to fly low and slow over your favorite cities because &#8212; if you pick the right one &#8212; every street and house will be there in more detail than you&#8217;ve ever seen in a game. Weather effects, day and night cycles, plane models &#8212; it all looks amazing. You can&#8217;t start it up and not fawn over the graphics.</p>\n<p>But the new Flight Simulator is also still very much a work in progress, too, even just a few weeks before the scheduled launch date on August 18. It&#8217;s officially still in beta, so there&#8217;s still time to fix at least some of the issues I list below. Because Microsoft and Asobo Studios, which was responsible for the development of the simulator, are using Microsoft&#8217;s AI tech in Azure to automatically generate much of the scenery based on Microsoft&#8217;s Bing Maps data, you&#8217;ll find a lot of weirdness in the world. There are taxiway lights in the middle of runways, giant hangars and crew buses at small private fields, cars randomly driving across airports, giant trees growing everywhere (while palms often look like giant sticks), bridges that are either under water or big blocks of black over a river &#8212; and there are a lot of sunken boats, too.</p>\n<p>When the system works well, it&#8217;s absolutely amazing. Cities like Barcelona, Berlin, San Francisco, Seattle, New York and others that are rendered using Microsoft&#8217;s photogrammetry method look great &#8212; including and maybe especially at night.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2023932\" style=\"width: 610px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-sedona.gif\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2023932\" class=\"wp-image-2023932 size-full\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-sedona.gif\" alt=\"\" width=\"600\" height=\"338\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2023932\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> Microsoft</p></div></p>\n<p>The rendering engine on my i7-9700K with an Nvidia 2070 Super graphics card never let the frame rate drop under 30 frames per second (which is perfectly fine for a flight simulator) and usually hovered well over 40, all with the graphics setting pushed up to the maximum and with a 2K resolution.</p>\n<p>When things don&#8217;t work, though, the effect is stark because it&#8217;s so obvious. Some cities, like Las Vegas, look like they suffered some kind of catastrophe, as if the city was abandoned and nature took over (which in the case of the Vegas Strip doesn&#8217;t sound like such a bad thing, to be honest).</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2023931\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-vegas.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2023931\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2023931\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-vegas.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"513\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-vegas.jpg 2019w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-vegas.jpg?resize=150,75 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-vegas.jpg?resize=300,150 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-vegas.jpg?resize=768,385 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-vegas.jpg?resize=680,341 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-vegas.jpg?resize=1536,769 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-vegas.jpg?resize=50,25 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2023931\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> TechCrunch</p></div></p>\n<p>Thankfully, all of this is something that Microsoft and Asobo can fix. They&#8217;ll just need to adjust their algorithms, and because a lot of the data is streamed, the updates should be virtually automatic. The fact that they haven&#8217;t done so yet is a bit of a surprise.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024258\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-145940.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024258\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2024258\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-145940.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"646\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-145940.jpg 1613w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-145940.jpg?resize=150,95 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-145940.jpg?resize=300,189 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-145940.jpg?resize=768,484 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-145940.jpg?resize=680,429 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-145940.jpg?resize=1536,968 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-145940.jpg?resize=50,32 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024258\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> TechCrunch</p></div></p>\n<p>Chances are you&#8217;ll want to fly over your house the day you get Flight Simulator. If you live in the right city (and the right part of that city), you&#8217;ll likely be lucky and actually see your house with its individual texture. But for some cities, including London, for example, the game only shows standard textures, and while Microsoft does a good job at matching the outlines of buildings in cities where it doesn&#8217;t do photogrammetry, it&#8217;s odd that London or Amsterdam aren&#8217;t on that list (though London apparently features a couple of wind turbines in the city center now), while Münster, Germany is.</p>\n<p>Once you get to altitude, all of those problems obviously go away (or at least you won&#8217;t see them). But given the graphics, you&#8217;ll want to spend a lot of time at 2,000 feet or below.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024149\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-124757.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024149\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2024149\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-124757.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"550\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-124757.jpg 1962w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-124757.jpg?resize=150,81 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-124757.jpg?resize=300,161 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-124757.jpg?resize=768,412 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-124757.jpg?resize=680,365 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-124757.jpg?resize=1536,824 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-124757.jpg?resize=50,27 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024149\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> TechCrunch</p></div></p>\n<p>What really struck me in playing the game in its current state is how those graphical inconsistencies set the standard for the rest of the experience. The team says its focus is 100% on making the simulator as realistic as possible, but then the virtual air traffic control often doesn&#8217;t use standard phraseology, for example, or fails to hand you off to the right departure control when you leave a major airport, for example. The airplane models look great and feel pretty close to real (at least for the ones I&#8217;ve flown myself), but some currently show the wrong airspeed, for example. Some planes use modern glass cockpits with the Garmin 1000 and G3X, but those still feel severely limited.</p>\n<p>But let me be clear here. Despite all of this, even in its beta state, Flight Simulator is a technical marvel and it will only get better over time.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2023929\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-palm-springs.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2023929\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2023929\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-palm-springs.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"506\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-palm-springs.jpg 1910w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-palm-springs.jpg?resize=150,74 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-palm-springs.jpg?resize=300,148 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-palm-springs.jpg?resize=768,379 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-palm-springs.jpg?resize=680,336 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-palm-springs.jpg?resize=1536,758 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-palm-springs.jpg?resize=50,25 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2023929\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> TechCrunch</p></div></p>\n<p>Let&#8217;s walk through the user experience a bit. The install on PC (the Xbox version will come at some point in the future) is a process that downloads a good 90GB so that you can play offline as well. The install process asks you if you are OK with streaming data, too, and that can quickly add up. After reinstalling the game and doing a few flights for screenshots, the game had downloaded about 10GB already &#8212; it adds up quickly and is something you should be aware of if you&#8217;re on a metered connection.</p>\n<p>[gallery ids=\"2024272,2024274,2024275,2024276,2024277,2024278,2024281\"]</p>\n<p>Once past the long install, you&#8217;ll be greeted by a menu screen that lets you start a new flight, go for one of the landing challenges or other activities the team has set up (they are really proud of their Courchevel scenery) and go through the games&#8217; flight training program.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2023926\" style=\"width: 810px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-world-map.gif\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2023926\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2023926\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-world-map.gif\" alt=\"\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2023926\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> Microsoft</p></div></p>\n<p>That training section walks you through eight activities that will help you get the basics of flying a Cessna 152. Most take fewer than 10 minutes and you&#8217;ll get a bit of a de-brief after, but I&#8217;m not sure it&#8217;s enough to keep a novice from getting frustrated quickly (while more advanced players will just skip this section altogether anyway).</p>\n<p>I mostly spent my time flying the small general aviation planes in the sim, but if you prefer a Boeing 747 or Airbus 320neo, you get that option, too, as well as some turboprops and business jets. I&#8217;ll spend some more time with those before the official launch. All of the planes are beautifully detailed inside and out and except for a few bugs, everything works as expected.</p>\n<p>To actually start playing, you&#8217;ll head for the world map and choose where you want to start your flight. What&#8217;s nice here is that you can pick any spot on your map, not just airports. That makes it easy to start flying over a city, for example. As you zoom into the map, you can see airports and landmarks (where the landmarks are either real sights like Germany&#8217;s Neuschwanstein Castle or cities that have photogrammetry data). If a town doesn&#8217;t have photogrammetry data, it will not appear on the map.</p>\n<p>As of now, the flight planning features are pretty basic. For visual flights, you can go direct or VOR to VOR, and that&#8217;s it. For IFR flights, you choose low or high-altitude airways. You can&#8217;t really adjust any of these, just accept what the simulator gives you. That&#8217;s not really how flight planning works (at the very least you would want to take the local weather into account), so it would be nice if you could customize your route a bit more. Microsoft partnered with NavBlue for airspace data, though the built-in maps don&#8217;t do much with this data and don&#8217;t even show you the vertical boundaries of the airspace you are in.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024150\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-123808-1.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024150\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2024150\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-123808-1.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"550\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-123808-1.jpg 1960w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-123808-1.jpg?resize=150,81 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-123808-1.jpg?resize=300,161 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-123808-1.jpg?resize=768,412 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-123808-1.jpg?resize=680,365 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-123808-1.jpg?resize=1536,824 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Annotation-2020-07-29-123808-1.jpg?resize=50,27 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024150\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> TechCrunch</p></div></p>\n<p>It&#8217;s always hard to compare the plane models and how they react to the real thing. Best I can tell, at least the single-engine Cessnas that I&#8217;m familiar with mostly handle in the same way I would expect them to in reality. Rudder controls feel a bit overly sensitive by default, but that&#8217;s relatively easy to adjust. I only played with a HOTAS-style joystick and rudder setup. I wouldn&#8217;t recommend playing with a mouse and keyboard, but your mileage may vary.</p>\n<p>Live traffic works well, but none of the general aviation traffic around my local airports seems to show up, even though Microsoft partner FlightAware shows it.</p>\n<p>As for the real/AI traffic in general, the sim does a pretty good job managing that. In the beta, you won&#8217;t really see the liveries of any real airlines yet &#8212; at least for the most part &#8212; I spotted the occasional United plane in the latest builds. Given some of Microsoft&#8217;s own videos, more are coming soon. Except for the built-in models you can fly in the sim, Flight Simulator is still missing a library of other airplane models for AI traffic, though again, I would assume that&#8217;s in the works, too.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024248\" style=\"width: 810px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-night-sf.gif\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024248\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2024248\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/ms-flight-night-sf.gif\" alt=\"\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024248\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> TechCrunch</p></div></p>\n<p>We&#8217;re three weeks out from launch. I would expect the team to be able to fix many of these issues and we&#8217;ll revisit all of them for our final review. My frustration with the current state of the game is that it&#8217;s so often so close to perfect that when it falls short of that, it&#8217;s especially jarring because it yanks you out of the experience.</p>\n<p>Don&#8217;t get me wrong, though, flying in FS2020 is already a great experience. Even when there&#8217;s no photogrammetry, cities and villages look great once you get over 3,000 feet or so. The weather and cloud simulation &#8212; in real time &#8212; beats any add-on for today&#8217;s flight simulators. Airports still need work, but having cars drive around and flaggers walking around planes that are pushing back help make the world feel more alive. Wind affects the waves on lakes and oceans (and windsocks on airports). This is truly a next-generation flight simulator.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2023939\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2023939\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2023939\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"576\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg 7680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg?resize=150,84 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg?resize=300,169 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg?resize=768,432 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg?resize=680,383 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg?resize=1536,864 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg?resize=2048,1152 2048w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/VL3_NYT_7K_Angkor.jpg?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2023939\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> Microsoft</p></div></p>\n<p>Microsoft and Asobo have to walk a fine line between making Flight Simulator the sim that hardcore fans want and an accessible game that brings in new players. I&#8217;ve played every version of Flight Simulator since the 90s, so getting started took exactly zero time. My sense is that new players simply looking for a good time may feel a bit lost at first, despite Microsoft adding landing challenges and other more gamified elements to the sim. In a press briefing, the Asobo team regularly stressed that it aimed for realism over anything else &#8212; and I&#8217;m perfectly ok with that. We&#8217;ll have to see if that translates to being a fun experience for casual players, too.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?a=JdixkwaHMQE:KxikrMezqtc:2mJPEYqXBVI\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a ff/Techcrunch?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn48@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596115828238_ae8500a395ae4c74.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=JdixkwaHMQE:KxikrMezqtc:-BTjWOF_DHI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn4@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596115829295_3ad70c8f3c34fa1b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=JdixkwaHMQE:KxikrMezqtc:D7DqB2pKExk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn48@2020_2/2020-07-30/1596115828229_d098beb5832ca4cb.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn92@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596115830290_749e2568e12b832a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/JdixkwaHMQE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn84@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596115829280_ebb70693733f2545.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-07-30 21:30:10","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 13:30:10","title":"Six leading investors assess the remote-work startup landscape","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2022974","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The COVID-19 pandemic has shaken up the startup world, </span><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/03/30/the-second-wave-of-unicorn-layoffs-is-brutal/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">slowing some high-growth unicorns</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> and promoting others onto the coveted list. In the earlier-stages of startup land, the same </span><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/07/17/extension-rounds-help-some-startups-play-offense-during-covid-19/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">patterns of acceleration and braking</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> can be found.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">TechCrunch wanted to dig more deeply into the cohort of startups that are seeing acceleration, so we put together a list of investors who have put money to work in startups building remote-work tooling and sent them a raft of questions. We wanted to better understand if SaaS fatigue is real for the startups in question, where open-space still exists in the remote-work world and how the economics of the companies compare to other software shops.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">And, yes, we did ask about valuations and intra-venture competition for the rounds that we keep hearing about. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Not every deal from these venture capitalists fits the remote-work mold, but they and their firms and funds are involved with enough in our view to give them good perspective about what’s going on in the space as the world continues to </span><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/07/15/as-companies-accelerate-their-digital-transitions-employees-detail-a-changed-workplace/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">get into shape</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> regarding remote work. This week’s </span><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/07/27/google-will-continue-to-let-employees-work-from-home-through-the-end-of-june-2021/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">news from Google</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> makes it plain that tech companies are prepping for a long run of forced remote work, let alone the more hybrid remote-and-office future that seems to be the new conventional expectation.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">A lasting </span><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/03/06/as-coronavirus-pandemic-spreads-demand-for-remote-work-startups-spikes/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">COVID-driven remote-work boom</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> is predicated on remote-work services not only meeting the moment, but iterating to support a world that just got pushed into a faster digital transformation than anyone expected. It’s a busy space for good reason.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Here’s who TechCrunch collected notes from this time around:</span></p>\n<ul>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://twitter.com/minney_cat\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Minn Kim</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, investor at </span><a href=\"https://www.bloombergbeta.com/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Bloomberg Beta</span></a></li>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://twitter.com/TheValuesVC\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Elliott Robinson</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, growth equity partner at </span><a href=\"https://www.bvp.com/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Bessemer Venture Partners</span></a></li>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://twitter.com/clementc\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Clement Cazalot</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, managing director of </span><a href=\"https://www.techstars.com/accelerators/boston\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Techstars Boston</span></a></li>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://twitter.com/ryankuder\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Ryan Kuder</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, </span><a href=\"https://www.techstars.com/accelerators/anywhere\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Techstars Anywhere</span></a></li>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://twitter.com/eamonncarey\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Eamonn Carey</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, managing director at </span><a href=\"https://www.techstars.com/accelerators/london\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Techstars London</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, partner at The Fund</span></li>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://twitter.com/alexavontobel\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Alexa von Tobel</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, founder and managing partner of </span><a href=\"https://inspiredcapital.com/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Inspired Capital</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">As usual, we’re going to riff over some key trends and themes that stood out from the group’s collected answers, after which we&#8217;ll share their answers at length, edited for clarity and formatting.</span></p>\n<h2>Trends, themes</h2>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Picking through the answers we received, one thing that stood out was the simple fact that VCs do not believe that the remote-work services and tooling world is solved. In fact, the group was not shy of suggesting areas where there’s still more work to be done.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">As you might expect, the issue of security came up a few times. Processes are being digitized across workplace verticals but there&#8217;s plenty of room for improvement in bringing security and compliance standards into the remote-work age. </span></p>\n<p>&#8220;All the security [and] compliance while being remote is still largely untapped as companies are figuring out the answer,&#8221; said Techstars&#8217; Cazalot, to pick one quote from a few.</p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Other items that popped up include front-of-office collaboration and personal remote process automation. </span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Once basic communications are sorted, the sorts of tools and services that folks-at-a-distance need to work well both alone and and as a team will be varied.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">It would be simple to presume that a growing library of apps and services would lead to software (SaaS) fatigue, but our VC group isn’t too worried about the concept.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Then there was the talk concerning economics and fundraising. TechCrunch wanted to know if remote-work startups have better or worse economics than other startups that are delivered along similar channels (SaaS, etc.). Our summary of answers it that their economics are at least as good, with some exceptions that their performance could in fact be <em>better </em>than other groupings of their business-model peers.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Regardless, the wave of companies hunting up new apps and services to fuel and empower their suddenly remote workforce is driving venture interest in the companies welcoming the demand. But notable is that prices, per our collected investors, are not as wild as you might think. Bessemer’s Robinson said prices were not low (&#8220;growth equity investors are paying high multiples to get a shot at the category defining [remote work-focused] app companies&#8221;), but most others offered some more tempered notes.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Reading their answers, it appears that the further an investor is from the Silicon Valley startup hub, the more reasonable prices look; that was likely true before the pandemic, mind, but that the pattern is holding up during COVID-19 implies that there is available price arbitrage available in the market that persists even into this hot niche.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">That’s our first read, but there’s a lot more below.</span></p>\n<h2><b>Minn Kim, </b><b>investor, Bloomberg Beta</b></h2>\n<h2><b style=\"font-family: 'Helvetica Neue', Helvetica, Arial, sans-serif; font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">It’s now common knowledge that the digital transformation has been accelerated by COVID-19. What portion of your active portfolio benefits from this change in pace?</b></h2>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Given our firm’s focus on the future of work, we’ve observed that a little over half of our portfolio companies have benefited from the digital transformation accelerated by COVID-19. These have been in areas like digital productivity, security, developer collaboration tools, network infrastructure and online education. This is also because several of our portfolio companies were built with a remote workforce in mind, including coaching platform</span><a href=\"https://www.soundingboardinc.com/\"> <span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Sounding Board</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> and team success software</span><a href=\"https://www.range.co/\"> <span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Range</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">.  </span></p>\n<p><b>Will the influx of single-purpose remote-work apps and services lead to app fatigue, and thus a return of more bundled solutions?</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">For those of us fortunate enough to do most of our work online, the rise of single-purpose remote-work apps reflects the limitations of the tools that we relied on in our previous “work stack.” If there is new bundling, I believe we may see it on platforms we’re newly relying on heavily, such as those offering great ways to run online sprints, remote offsites, and collaborative screen sharing.  </span></p>\n<p><b>Is there an upper limit to the number of tools that a single company may want to buy? Put another way, is SaaS fatigue real?</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">An upper limit for tool purchasing is more likely indication that a product is not yet hitting on the most important priority for its customer at the right time or price. SaaS fatigue is real until a solution comes along that addresses the user’s pain point in a clear, compelling and differentiated way. </span></p>\n<p><b>As companies begin to go back to the office, do you think they are going to trend back toward their old processes and ditch some of their new remote software?</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">For knowledge work, I believe some behaviors will naturally default back to old processes, such as in-person water cooler conversations in a shared common space. Even when companies begin to go back to the office, I imagine some people on our teams will continue to be remote, at least for awhile. With this in mind, I foresee an ongoing reliance on tools like</span><a href=\"http://www.pinpoint.com/\"> <span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Pinpoint</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, for helping software engineers collaborate more efficiently, and</span><a href=\"https://bonus.ly/\"> <span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Bonusly</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, for ongoing encouragement and recognition to team members (in-person and remote).</span></p>\n<p><b>Are there areas inside of the world of remote tools and services that you think are under-served, places where you’d like to place a bet?</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">I’d love to see more founders building solutions around lightweight automations (“personal RPA”), software for better decision-making and products that use digital nudges to make unstructured collaboration more effective, such as gesture recognition or sentiment notifications in video conversations. There are also many opportunities to build useful products that use the metadata in our online interactions to help us become more effective teammates. For example, we’re investors in a company called</span><a href=\"https://cultivate.com/\"> <span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Cultivate</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> that analyzes a team’s digital conversations to help leaders improve and get notified when they’re sending too many emails during evening hours or sharing recognition unevenly across a team. </span></p>\n<p><b>Does the availability of ample private capital give remote-tooling startups the flexibility to put off going up-market to the mid-market and enterprise areas?</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">It’s less about the flexibility of delaying moving up-market. It’s more about the freedom to experiment, iterate and get a product closer to product-market-fit with its earliest representative customer segment. </span></p>\n<p><b>Do remote-tooling-focused startups have similar, better or worse economics than the average venture-backed SaaS startup?</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">This answer will vary depending on what you’re comparing. Because many of the newer remote-tooling-focused companies offer freemium business models to encourage new users, the sustainability of a company comes down to the cost to serve each new customer. We’ve seen that remote-tooling-focused startups that are laser-focused on a specific use case and narrow the pool of potential early customers are often in a better position to understand their unit economics. </span></p>\n<p><b>How competitive are remote-work tooling venture rounds now? Are prices out of control?</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">It feels like the right time to build and raise for a Zoom competitor was at the beginning of 2020. Now, we’re seeing competitive rounds for companies building on top of Zoom or around workflow-specific features, such as event and community management or meeting analytics. That said, prices at the pre-seed and seed stages haven’t changed dramatically since pre-Covid-19. </span></p>\n<h2><b>Elliott Robinson, growth equity partner, Bessemer<div 21:30:10","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 13:30:10","title":"GM starts construction on the cornerstone of its EV strategy","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2024160","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Steel construction has begun on the nearly 3-million-square-foot factory that will mass produce Ultium battery cells and packs, the cornerstone of General Motors&#8217; strategy to bring 20 electric vehicles to market by 2023.</p>\n<p>The Ultium Cells LLC battery cell manufacturing facility in Lordstown, Ohio is part of a joint venture between GM and LG Chem that was announced in December. At the time, the two companies committed to invest up $2.3 billion into the new joint venture as well as establish a battery cell assembly plant on a greenfield manufacturing site in the Lordstown area of Northeast Ohio that will create more than 1,100 new jobs. The factory will be able produce 30 gigawatts hours of capacity annually. To put that into perspective, Tesla&#8217;s factory in Sparks, Nevada, which is part of a partnership with Panasonic, has a 35 GW hour capacity.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024218\" style=\"width: 610px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024218\" class=\"wp-image-2024218 size-full\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg\" alt=\"GM Ultium Cells factory\" width=\"600\" height=\"400\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg 600w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg?resize=50,33 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 600px) 100vw, 600px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024218\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">Construction at the all-new Ultium Cells LLC battery cell manufacturing facility in Lordstown, Ohio.</p></div></p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>The batteries and an underlying electric architecture will be used in a broad range of products across its Cadillac, Buick, Chevrolet and GMC brands as well as the Cruise Origin autonomous shuttle that was revealed in January. The Cadillac Lyriq EV flagship and an all-electric GMC Hummer, which will be revealed this fall and go into production in the fourth quarter of 2021, will use the Ultium battery system. GM plans to reveal the Lyriq at a virtual event August 6.</p>\n<p>This modular architecture, called “Ultium,” (same as the battery) will be capable of 19 different battery and drive unit configurations, 400-volt and 800-volt packs with storage ranging from 50 kWh to 200 kWh, and front-, rear- and all-wheel drive configurations. At the heart of the new modular architecture, will be the large-format pouch battery cells manufactured this new factory.</p>\n<p>GM broke ground at the factory site in May and has since poured the concrete footings for the facility. Steel construction will continue into fall 2020, according to GM.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024256\" style=\"width: 610px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024256\" class=\"wp-image-2024256 size-full\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg\" alt=\"GM Ultium Battery Cell Manufacturing Factory\" width=\"600\" height=\"400\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg 600w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg?resize=50,33 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 600px) 100vw, 600px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024256\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> GM</p></div></p>\n<p>GM has used LG Chem as a lithium-ion and electronics supplier for at least a decade. The companies began working together in 2009. The relationship deepened as GM developed and then launched the Chevy Bolt EV.</p>\n<p>This latest joint venture marks a step change for GM and a means to accelerating its EV plans, which could even involve <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/reuters/2020/07/29/business/29reuters-gm-results-evs.html\">spinning out the operations</a> into a separate company.</p>\n<p class=\"css-158dogj evys1bk0\">&#8220;We are open to looking at and evaluate anything that we think is going to drive long-term shareholder value, so I would say nothing is off the table,&#8221; GM Chairman and CEO Mary Barra said during an earnings call Wednesday.</p>\n<p>GM reported Wednesday a <a href=\"https://media.gm.com/content/dam/Media/gmcom/investor/2020/jul/gm-q2-2020-earnings-press-release.pdf\">$758 million loss in the second quarter</a> on $16.78 billion in revenue. The loss and the 53% year-over-year drop in revenue was caused mostly by COVID-19-related factory shutdowns in the U.S. However, the results still managed to beat analysts expectations.</p>\n<div class=\"contributor-byline__bio\">\n\t\t<a href=\"http://willrobbins.org/\">Will Robbins</a> is an early-stage investor at <a href=\"http://contrarycap.com/\">Contrary</a>.\n\t</div>\n\t\n\t\t<div class=\"contributor-byline__more-articles\">\n\t\t<span class=\"more-articles-title\">More posts by this contributor</span>\n\t\t<ul class=\"more-articles-list\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<li><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/02/17/voodoo-games-thrives-by-upending-conventional-product-design/\">Voodoo Games thrives by upending conventional product design</a></li>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<li><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/02/03/venture-investing-in-eldertech/\">Venture investing in elder tech</a></li>\n\t\t\t\t\t</ul>\n\t</div>\n\t</div>\n</div><p id=\"speakable-summary\">There are more than 300,000 congregations in the U.S., and entrepreneurs are creating billion-dollar companies by building software to service them. Welcome to church tech.</p>\n<p>The sector was growing prior to COVID-19, but the pandemic forced many congregations to go entirely online, which rapidly accelerated growth in this space. While many of these companies were bootstrapped, VC dollars are also increasingly flowing in. Unfortunately, it&#8217;s hard to come across a lot of resources covering this expanding, unique sector.</p>\n<h2>Market map</h2>\n<p>In broad terms, we can split church tech into six categories:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>church management software (ChMS)</li>\n<li>digital giving</li>\n<li>member outreach/messaging</li>\n<li>streaming/content</li>\n<li>Bible study</li>\n<li>website and app building</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Horizontal integration is huge in this sector, and nearly all the companies operating in this space fall into several of these categories. Many have expanded through M&#38;A.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024180\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024180\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-2024180\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Church_market_map.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"929\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Church_market_map.jpg 2000w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Church_market_map.jpg?resize=150,136 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Church_market_map.jpg?resize=300,272 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Church_market_map.jpg?resize=768,697 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Church_market_map.jpg?resize=680,617 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Church_market_map.jpg?resize=1536,1393 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/Church_market_map.jpg?resize=50,45 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024180\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> <a href=\"https://contrarycap.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Contrary Capital <span class=\"screen-reader-text\">(opens in a new window)</span></a></p></div></p>\n<h2>The categories</h2>\n<ul>\n<li><strong>Church management software:</strong> Almost all are SaaS businesses, mostly using cloud hosting. Typical features include workflow management, virtual check-in for events, a database of members and online scheduling. Examples include <a href=\"https://www.elvanto.com/us/\">Elvanto</a> and <a href=\"https://www.onechurchsoftware.com/\">One Church</a>.<div class=\"extra-crunch-offer-container\"></div><div 21:30:10","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 13:30:10","title":"Zuckerberg unconvincingly feigns ignorance of data-sucking VPN scandal","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2024285","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Facebook&#8217;s <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/person/mark-zuckerberg\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"person\" data-entity=\"mark-zuckerberg\">Mark Zuckerberg <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> appeared less than entirely truthful at today&#8217;s House Judiciary hearing, regarding last year&#8217;s major Onavo controversy, in which his company paid teenagers to use a VPN app that reported detailed data on their internet use. Though he may not have outright lied about it, his answers were evasive and misleading enough to warrant a rushed clarification shortly afterward.</p>\n<p>Rep. Hank Johnson (D-GA) was asking Zuckerberg to confirm a series events last year first reported by TechCrunch: A VPN app called Onavo, owned by Facebook, was <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/08/22/apple-facebook-onavo/\">kicked out of Apple&#8217;s App Store</a> for collecting and reporting usage data while purporting to provide a protective service.</p>\n<p>Soon afterward, Facebook quietly <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/01/29/facebook-project-atlas/\">began paying people — 18 percent of whom were teenagers — to install the &#8220;Facebook Research&#8221; app</a>, which did much the same thing as Onavo under a different name. TechCrunch reported this and <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/01/30/apple-bans-facebook-vpn/\">Apple issued a ban</a> before the end of that day; Facebook claimed to have removed it voluntarily, but this was shown not to be true.</p>\n<p>Rep. Johnson questioned Zuckerberg along these lines, and the latter repeatedly expressed his unsureness about and lack of familiarity with these issues.</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Johnson</strong>: When it became public that Facebook was using Onavo to conduct digital surveillance, your company got kicked out of Apple&#8217;s App store, isn&#8217;t that true?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I&#8217;m not sure I&#8217;d characterize it in that way.</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: I mean, Onavo did get kicked out of the app store, isn&#8217;t that true?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, we took the app out after Apple changed their policies on VPN apps.</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: And it was because of the use of the surveillance tools.</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I&#8217;m not sure the policy was worded that way or that it&#8217;s exactly the right characterization of it&#8230; [The policies are explained below.]</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: Let me ask you this question, after Onavo was booted out of the app store, you turned to other surveillance tools, such as Facebook Research App, correct?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, in general, yes, we do a broad variety—</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: Isn&#8217;t it true, Mr. Zuckerberg, that Facebook paid teenagers to sell their privacy by installing Facebook Research App?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I&#8217;m not familiar with that, but I think it&#8217;s a general practice that companies use to, uh, have different surveys and understand data from how people are using different products and what their preferences are.</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: Facebook Research app got thrown out of the App Store too, isn&#8217;t that true?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I&#8217;m not familiar with that.</p></blockquote>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024315\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024315\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2024315\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"610\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg 1494w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg?resize=150,89 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg?resize=680,405 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg?resize=50,30 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024315\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> YouTube</p></div></p>\n<p>Of course, the idea that Zuckerberg was not familiar with events that made headlines, took down Facebook&#8217;s internal apps for days, and prompted an angry letter to him from a senator <a href=\"https://twitter.com/JoshConstine/status/1288595894435106817\">is absurd</a>. (After all, Facebook <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/02/28/facebook-research-teens/\">responded</a>.)</p>\n<p>Perhaps intuiting that this particular claim of ignorance was a bridge too far (and perhaps in response to some frantic off-screen action in the CEO&#8217;s barnlike virtual testimony HQ), Zuckerberg took the opportunity to backpedal a few minutes later:</p>\n<blockquote><p>In response to Congressman Johnson’s question, before I said that I wasn’t familiar with the Facebook research app when I wasn’t familiar with that name for it. I just want to be clear that I do recall we used an app for research and it’s since been discontinued.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Of course, although Zuckerberg may plausibly have been unsure about the name, it&#8217;s not to be believed that he was not familiar with the events of that time, as they were both highly publicized and very costly for Facebook. Naturally he would also have been refreshed on them during preparation for this testimony.</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"yAv9cnRVkC\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/01/30/facebook-research-app/\">Senator Warner calls on Zuckerberg to support market research consent rules</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" title=\"&#8220;Senator Warner calls on Zuckerberg to support market research consent rules&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/01/30/facebook-research-app/embed/#?secret=yAv9cnRVkC\" data-secret=\"yAv9cnRVkC\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe></div>\n<p>That Zuckerberg is unfamiliar with the exact wording of Apple&#8217;s rules is possible, even probable, but it was no secret that the rules were changed basically in response to reports of Facebook&#8217;s Onavo shenanigans. Here is what Apple said at the time:</p>\n<blockquote><p>We work hard to protect user privacy and data security throughout the Apple ecosystem. With the latest update to our guidelines, we made it explicitly clear that apps should not collect information about which other apps are installed on a user’s device for the purposes of analytics or advertising/marketing and must make it clear what user data will be collected and how it will be used.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Later, when TechCrunch showed that Facebook had been using an enterprise deployment tool to essentially sideload spyware onto teenagers&#8217; phones, Apple said this:</p>\n<blockquote><p>We designed our Enterprise Developer Program solely for the internal distribution of apps within an organization. Facebook has been using their membership to distribute a data-collecting app to consumers, which is a clear breach of their agreement with Apple. Any developer using their enterprise certificates to distribute apps to consumers will have their certificates revoked, which is what we did in this case to protect our users and their data.</p></blockquote>\n<p>So Facebook was the reason, implicitly first, then later explicitly, for these App Store lockdowns. Rep. Johnson put the whole thing quite plainly at the end of his questions.</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Johnson</strong>: You tried one thing and then you got caught, made some apologies, then you did it all over again. [long pause]&#8230; Isn&#8217;t that true?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I respectfully disagree with that characterization.</p></blockquote>\n<p>You can watch the full hearing 21:30:10","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 13:30:10","title":"Connected audio was a bad choice","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2024142","description":"<p class=\"p3\">The past week, I’ve spent ample time looking to revamp my home audio setup. I think my only qualification is that my next setup is as dumb as possible.</p>\n<p id=\"speakable-summary\">In the past five years, my setup has gone from a fairly middling wired 2.1 speaker setup to a confusing menagerie of connected smart speakers. I&#8217;ve likely gone through at least five <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/google\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"google\">Google <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> Assistant-laden speakers including the Google Home Max, a couple connected <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/sonos\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"sonos\">Sonos <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> speakers, three HomePods, a Facebook Portal+, non-smart speakers connected via Chromecast Audio and god knows how many Alexa-integrated speakers. All in all, I can firmly say I have made some very bad audio decisions in my recent life.</p>\n<p>I&#8217;ve had a lot of frustrations with my current setup, but they&#8217;re really issues with the entire smart speaker market:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Good audio hardware should be timeless, and devices that need frequent firmware updates, have proprietary support for a certain operating system or can lose integration support quickly fly in the face of that.</li>\n<li>Home entertainment integrations with these speakers are just awful, even among products built by the same company. Repeatedly connecting my stereo HomePods to my Apple TV has been maddening.</li>\n<li>Smart assistants are much less ambitious than they were years ago and the ceiling of innovation already seems to have come down significantly. Third party integrations have sunk far below expectations and it&#8217;s pretty uncertain that these voice interfaces have as bright a future as these tech companies once hoped.</li>\n<li>These assistants were once going to be the operating systems of the home, but the smart home experiment largely feels like a failure and it&#8217;s growing clearer that the dream of a Jarvis-like system that plays nicely with all of your internet-connected devices was totally naive.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>All in all, it&#8217;s time for me to move on and invest some cash in a setup that will sound good for decades.</p>\n<p>Now, many of you will say that my true error was a lack of commitment to one ecosystem, which is undoubtedly spot-on and yet I don’t think any of the players had precisely what I wanted hence the wildly piecemeal approach. Dumping more funds into a robust Sonos setup probably would have been the wisest commitment, but I have commitment issues and I think part of it was a desire to see what was out there.</p>\n<p>In quarantine, I’ve gotten ample time to spend with my home audio system and the destructive weave on non-compatible hardware is all too much. I don&#8217;t want my speakers to have their own operating systems or for one speaker to play nice with my music streaming platform of choice, but not the other. I want something that can last.</p>\n<p>After doing half-commits to several ecosystems, I feel I&#8217;ve seen and heard it all and now I&#8217;m shopping for some good old-fashioned dumb wired surround sound speakers to integrate with a <em>slightly</em> smarter AV receiver. God willing, I will have strength to not buy whatever cool audio gadgets come out next year and can stay strong. If you have some good tips on a nice setup, please help me out.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?a=snAMqEjuYFA:14ycr5ZEGAM:2mJPEYqXBVI\"><img
